[Title & Abstract] generating (no tool calls)...

Spatial Inequality in Fine Particulate Exposure and Income Inequality Across California Counties in 2019

Abstract

Background and Purpose: County-level fine particulate matter (PM2.5) exposure and income inequality vary markedly across California, yet empirical assessments are often limited by uneven monitoring coverage and by the need to evaluate spatial dependence explicitly. This study quantifies 2019 county PM2.5 exposure patterns, links them to county income inequality (Gini index), and establishes a spatial-analytic framework to examine whether associations and residual structure are spatially patterned, including by urban–rural status. Methods: Monitor-based annual PM2.5 records were filtered to 2019 and aggregated to county means using Federal Information Processing Standards geographic identifiers, then joined to 2019 California county polygons (58 counties). Income inequality was measured using the 2019 county Gini index (joined at 100% match), and spatial relationships were operationalized using Queen contiguity weights (mean neighborhood size 4.79; range 2–8) and a centroid-based $k$-nearest-neighbors graph ($k=5$). Ordinary least squares fitted values and residuals were appended at the county level to support subsequent spatial autocorrelation diagnostics and spatial regression specifications. Results: Monitor-derived county PM2.5 means were available for 44 of 58 counties (75.9%), leaving 14 counties (24.1%) without a county mean in 2019. Among counties with PM2.5 data, mean exposure was 7.45 $\mu g/m^3$ (SD 2.14), spanning from 3.14 to 12.90 $\mu g/m^3$, and monitoring intensity varied from 1 to 19 sites per county (mean 3.39). The county Gini index was observed for all 58 counties, with a mean of 0.459 (SD 0.025) and a range from 0.400 to 0.509. The analytical dataset therefore supports joint modeling of PM2.5 and inequality for 44 counties with complete exposure information while retaining statewide inequality and classification attributes for comparative mapping and stratified interpretation. Conclusions: Results indicate substantial cross-county heterogeneity in PM2.5 exposure and income inequality in 2019, alongside nontrivial missingness in monitor-derived PM2.5 that is consequential for spatial inference. By combining county polygons with explicit contiguity- and distance-based weights, the study provides a reproducible basis for testing spatial clustering, estimating inequality–exposure relationships, and evaluating whether spatial structure persists beyond a non-spatial baseline, including under urban–rural stratification.

Keywords: PM2.5 exposure; income inequality; Gini index; spatial autocorrelation; Queen contiguity weights; county-level analysis; California

[Introduction] finalizing (tool budget exhausted)...

1. Introduction

Fine particulate matter (PM2.5) remains a central environmental-health and environmental-justice concern because exposure is spatially uneven and closely tied to the economic and infrastructural systems that organize everyday life. These inequalities matter not only for public health, but also for climate and energy transitions that can shift emission sources and alter the distribution of exposures over space and across populations (Watts et al., 2020) [REF_6]. A large body of environmental justice research further emphasizes that pollution burdens are not randomly distributed; instead, they frequently align with social stratification and place-based disadvantage, making the identification of where disparities occur—and how they relate to economic inequality—a key scientific and policy question (Banzhaf et al., 2019) [REF_7].

Prior work provides two critical building blocks for studying PM2.5 and inequality in a spatial-analytic framework. First, spatial data on environmental and socioeconomic conditions routinely exhibit spatial dependence, and failing to account for this structure can bias inference or misstate uncertainty (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]. Spatial econometrics has therefore developed a set of diagnostics and model families—such as spatial lag and spatial error specifications—to distinguish diffusion-like dependence in outcomes from spatially correlated unobservables and to improve estimation in place-based analyses (Anselin, 1988) [REF_17]; (Elhorst, 2011) [REF_18]. Second, applied social-science research increasingly treats “space” as an explicit component of the data-generating process, motivating careful choices about spatial proximity (e.g., contiguity versus distance-based graphs) and about how to evaluate residual spatial autocorrelation following non-spatial baselines (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22]. These methodological perspectives are especially relevant when linking county-level environmental exposures to county-level inequality indicators, where adjacent jurisdictions often share airsheds, commuting systems, and policy regimes. In California specifically, questions about who benefits and who bears burdens under decarbonization and electrification pathways have been explicitly framed as environmental-justice issues, underscoring the value of spatially explicit county-scale evidence for planning and accountability (Hettinger et al., 2021) [REF_4]. Moreover, California’s heterogeneous urban form and transportation systems can generate sharp spatial gradients in exposure that motivate county comparisons and metropolitan versus non-metropolitan stratification (Severen, 2021) [REF_15].

Despite these foundations, important evidence gaps remain at the intersection of air pollution, income inequality, and spatial dependence—gaps that are directly consequential for how associations are interpreted and how policy-relevant “hotspots” are identified. Although (Chang et al., 2019) [REF_8] estimates the effect of pollution on worker productivity using detailed microdata, it is confined to a non-U.S. setting and does not address whether pollution–inequality relationships are spatially structured across local jurisdictions, leaving county-scale spatial inequality in exposure underexplored. Although (Chen et al., 2021) [REF_10] synthesizes cardiovascular health impacts of wildfire smoke exposure, it does not evaluate how broader economic inequality relates to ambient particulate exposure patterns or whether any observed associations are confounded by spatial dependence, leaving the role of income inequality in shaping spatial exposure gradients underexplored. Although (Bleaney and Nishiyama, 2004) [REF_1] examines how the relationship between income inequality and macroeconomic outcomes can vary with context, it does not connect inequality to environmental exposure processes or to spatially explicit environmental-justice metrics, leaving the question of how income inequality covaries with PM2.5 exposure across space underexplored. Together, these limitations motivate an explicitly spatial county-level assessment that (i) treats exposure as a geographic surface sampled unevenly in space, (ii) tests for spatial clustering directly, and (iii) evaluates whether a non-spatial regression leaves spatially patterned residual structure that would call for spatial econometric specifications.

This study addresses these issues for California in 2019 by asking: How do spatial inequalities in PM2.5 exposure across California counties vary with income inequality (Gini) in 2019, and does the spatial relationship vary by urban–rural classification? The analysis is organized around three objectives aligned with a reproducible GIScience workflow. Objective 1 assesses how county-level annual PM2.5 varies across California and tests whether county PM2.5 is spatially clustered using global spatial autocorrelation diagnostics based on contiguity weights. Objective 2 estimates the county-level association between PM2.5 and income inequality (Gini index) using an ordinary least squares (OLS) baseline and then evaluates whether residual spatial autocorrelation remains—an empirical signal that a spatial regression specification may be more appropriate (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]; (Anselin, 1988) [REF_17]. To operationalize spatial dependence, the study uses both polygon contiguity and centroid-based distance graphs and fits maximum-likelihood spatial lag and spatial error models consistent with standard spatial-econometric practice (Elhorst, 2011) [REF_18]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22]. Objective 3 tests whether the PM2.5–inequality association differs by metropolitan status via interaction modeling, reflecting the expectation that economic inequality may map onto exposure differently in metropolitan versus non-metropolitan county contexts (Hettinger et al., 2021) [REF_4]. Across objectives, the study emphasizes transparent construction of geographic identifiers for county joins, explicit definition of spatial weights, and diagnostic checking of residual dependence as core elements of spatially defensible inference (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19]. More broadly, it contributes a county-scale, place-based environmental inequality framework that connects exposure measurement, spatial autocorrelation diagnostics, and spatial econometric model comparison in a single workflow, complementing ongoing methodological attention to spatial correlation and inference in geographically indexed data (DellaVigna et al., 2025) [REF_3].

H1: County-level annual PM2.5 exposure in 2019 is spatially clustered (positive global Moran’s I) rather than randomly distributed across California. This hypothesis follows from the general expectation that environmental conditions, including air pollution, are spatially structured by shared meteorology, regional emissions, and cross-boundary transport, making spatial autocorrelation a common feature of areal environmental datasets (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]. H2: Higher county income inequality (B19083_001E:Gini Index) is associated with higher county mean PM2.5 (pm25_mean_2019) in 2019, and OLS residuals exhibit spatial autocorrelation indicating the need for a spatial regression specification. This hypothesis is consistent with environmental-justice perspectives emphasizing that socio-economic stratification can align with pollution burdens (Banzhaf et al., 2019) [REF_7] and with spatial-econometric arguments that ignoring spatial dependence can leave structured residuals that undermine standard regression assumptions (Anselin, 1988) [REF_17]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22]. H3: The association between B19083_001E:Gini Index and pm25_mean_2019 differs by Metro2013 status (effect modification). This hypothesis is motivated by the notion that metropolitan and non-metropolitan counties may differ systematically in emission sources, transportation systems, and governance constraints, and that California’s decarbonization and electrification trajectories raise explicit distributional questions that can plausibly vary across urban–rural contexts (Hettinger et al., 2021) [REF_4].

The remainder of this paper is organized as follows. Section 2 describes data sources, county identifier construction, and the spatial-weights definitions used to represent contiguity and distance-based proximity. Section 3 presents the methods for mapping and diagnosing spatial clustering, estimating OLS and spatial regression models, and evaluating residual spatial autocorrelation, including the urban–rural interaction framework. Section 4 reports the empirical results, including spatial patterns, model estimates, and diagnostic tests. Section 5 discusses implications for spatial environmental inequality research and for California-relevant environmental-justice planning, alongside limitations and directions for future work. Section 6 concludes.

2. Methodology

2.1 Study Area

The study was conducted for the 58 counties of the State of California (United States), using county polygons as the areal unit of analysis. California provided a policy-relevant and data-supported setting for examining spatial inequality in ambient PM2.5 because air quality and environmental-justice concerns are routinely framed at county and regional scales, while the state’s heterogeneous metropolitan structure creates strong spatial gradients in emissions, exposure, and socioeconomic conditions (Hettinger et al., 2021) [REF_4]; (Severen, 2021) [REF_15]. The analysis focused on calendar year 2019 to align annual PM2.5 monitoring summaries with a single-year county income-inequality measure.

2.2 Data

We integrated four primary data objects: (i) monitoring-site annual PM2.5 summaries, (ii) county boundary polygons, (iii) county income inequality (Gini index), and (iv) a metropolitan/non-metropolitan classification appended at the county level. All spatial data products used geographic coordinates in EPSG:4269.

PM2.5 monitoring-site annual summaries (input table). PM2.5 data were sourced from PM2.5_Annual_California_2019.csv (path: /bigdata/s0/tea5209/AGM/outputs/DataRetrieverOutput/PM2.5_Annual_California_2019.csv), a CSV file with shape [1291, 56]. The fields used downstream were state_code, county_code, site_number, year, pollutant_standard, and arithmetic_mean (Data Registry). These records represented monitoring sites rather than counties; county-level exposure metrics were therefore constructed by aggregation.

California county boundaries (input polygons). County geometries were taken from california_counties_2019.gpkg (path: /bigdata/s0/tea5209/AGM/outputs/DataRetrieverOutput/california_counties_2019.gpkg), then carried through subsequent derived GeoPackages. Downstream county layers consistently contained standard county identifiers including STATEFP, COUNTYFP, GEOID, and county attributes ALAND and AWATER. The derived county layers used EPSG:4269 (Output File Statistics).

County income inequality (Gini) (input table). County Gini values were sourced from California_Counties_Gini_2019.csv (path: /bigdata/s0/tea5209/AGM/outputs/DataRetrieverOutput/California_Counties_Gini_2019.csv, per Research Plan) and processed to retain state_fips, county_fips, year, and B19083_001E:Gini Index. The join-ready file gini_2019_with_geoid.csv was created as an intermediate output (Execution Trace, Objective 2).

Metropolitan status (derived attribute). A derived county GeoPackage, counties_pm25_gini_urbanrural_2019.gpkg, contained a metropolitan status attribute Metro2013 appended to the county layer that already included pm25_mean_2019 and B19083_001E:Gini Index (Output File Statistics). Because the execution trace for the construction of Metro2013 was not included in the provided trace block, we reported its presence and column name exactly as stored in the derived file, but we did not infer additional details (e.g., source, coding rules, or year) beyond what was explicitly available in the outputs.

Preprocessing and data integration

We constructed a county-level PM2.5 exposure indicator for 2019 by filtering the monitoring-site table to the annual standard and year used in the study and then aggregating to county identifiers.

First, we filtered PM2.5_Annual_California_2019.csv to rows with year == 2019 and pollutant_standard == 'PM25 Annual 2006', retaining the four columns state_code, county_code, site_number, and arithmetic_mean (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 1; output: pm25_2019_annual_records.csv). Second, we derived a 5-character county Federal Information Processing Standards identifier GEOID by concatenating a zero-padded 2-digit state code and a zero-padded 3-digit county code (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 2; output: pm25_2019_with_geoid.csv). Formally, for each record,
$$
\texttt{GEOID} = \text{zfill}_2(\texttt{state_code}) \,\Vert\, \text{zfill}_3(\texttt{county_code}),
$$
where $\Vert$ denotes string concatenation and $\text{zfill}_k(\cdot)$ indicates left zero-padding to width $k$.

Third, we aggregated monitoring-site observations to counties by grouping on GEOID and computing (i) pm25_mean_2019 as the mean of arithmetic_mean and (ii) pm25_site_count_2019 as the count of site_number records (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 3; output: pm25_county_2019.csv). Fourth, we left-joined the county-level PM2.5 table to the county polygons on GEOID (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 4; output: counties_pm25_2019.gpkg). The join audit for this step documented 58 county polygons on the left and a match rate of 44/58 (75.9%) for non-null pm25_mean_2019, leaving 14 counties unmatched (i.e., without monitor-derived annual PM2.5 summaries under the specified filter) (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 4).

To make missingness explicit and reproducible in subsequent analysis, we derived a boolean indicator pm25_missing_2019 defined as (pm25_mean_2019 is null) (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 5; output: counties_pm25_2019_qc.gpkg). The QC log reported 14/58 counties (24.1%) missing pm25_mean_2019 (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 5). These missing values were not imputed; instead, downstream statistical procedures that required a dependent variable (e.g., regression, Moran’s I for PM2.5) operated on complete cases only, as reflected by non-null counts in derived outputs (e.g., pm25_mean_2019 count of 44 in counties_pm25_2019_qc.gpkg and OLS output fields populated for 44 counties in counties_pm25_gini_2019_olsresid.gpkg; Output File Statistics).

For inequality, we filtered the county Gini table to year == 2019 (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 1; output: gini_2019_filtered.csv), then derived GEOID by concatenating zero-padded state and county FIPS codes (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 2; output: gini_2019_with_geoid.csv). We then left-joined the Gini attribute into the county PM2.5 QC layer on GEOID (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 3; output: counties_pm25_gini_2019.gpkg). The join audit reported 58/58 matched values for B19083_001E:Gini Index (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 3), yielding a spatial dataset with complete Gini coverage but incomplete PM2.5 coverage (pm25_missing_2019).

No CRS reprojection was recorded in the execution trace; derived spatial layers and weights files reported EPSG:4269 (Output File Statistics). Consequently, contiguity-based neighborhood structure was computed directly from polygon topology in EPSG:4269, and centroid-based distance neighborhoods (KNN) were computed from centroid coordinates in EPSG:4269 as well (see Methods).

2.3 Methods

Analytical framework

We implemented a two-part spatial-analytic workflow consistent with the study aims: (i) characterize whether county PM2.5 exposure exhibited global spatial clustering and (ii) estimate the bivariate association between PM2.5 exposure and county income inequality while diagnosing whether residual spatial dependence remained after a non-spatial baseline. This sequencing followed standard guidance that spatial dependence should be diagnosed explicitly and, when present, incorporated into model specification or inference to avoid misleading conclusions (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]; (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19]. In addition, we operationalized “spatial proximity” using two common neighborhood graphs—polygon contiguity and centroid-based K-nearest neighbors—to reflect different plausible channels of interdependence across counties (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22].

Global spatial autocorrelation of county PM2.5 (Moran’s I)

To test whether county PM2.5 exposure was spatially clustered, we computed global Moran’s I using a Queen contiguity neighborhood graph built from county polygons (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]. Let $y_i$ denote county $i$’s annual mean PM2.5 exposure pm25_mean_2019 for the set of counties with non-missing values (complete cases), $n$ the number of such counties, and $w_{ij}$ the $(i,j)$ entry of the spatial weights matrix $W$ derived from Queen contiguity. Moran’s I was computed as
$$
I = \frac{n}{\sum_{i}\sum_{j} w_{ij}}
\frac{\sum_{i}\sum_{j} w_{ij}(y_i - \bar{y})(y_j - \bar{y})}{\sum_{i}(y_i - \bar{y})^2},
$$
where $\bar{y}$ is the mean of $y_i$ over complete cases. Operationally, we constructed Queen contiguity weights from the county geometries in counties_pm25_2019_qc.gpkg, keyed by GEOID (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 6; output: ca_county_queen_weights_2019.gpkg). We then computed global Moran’s I for pm25_mean_2019 using these Queen weights (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 7; output: pm25_global_moransI_2019.csv). The execution trace did not report additional options (e.g., weight standardization, permutation count); therefore, we only documented the neighborhood definition and key field used (GEOID) as the reproducible spatial specification.

Because 14 of 58 counties lacked pm25_mean_2019 under the monitor-based aggregation, Moran’s I was necessarily computed on the subset with observed PM2.5 values, consistent with the non-null count of pm25_mean_2019 (44) recorded in the derived county layers (Output File Statistics). No additional sensitivity analyses for missingness were executed in the trace.

Baseline OLS association between PM2.5 and income inequality, and residual spatial dependence

We estimated the association between county PM2.5 exposure and income inequality using an ordinary least squares (OLS) baseline, then tested residual spatial autocorrelation using global Moran’s I on the OLS residuals to diagnose remaining spatial structure (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]; (Anselin, 1988) [REF_17]. The OLS model was specified as
$$
y_i = \beta_0 + \beta_1 x_i + \varepsilon_i, \qquad \varepsilon_i \sim \mathcal{N}(0,\sigma^2),
$$
where $y_i$ was county $i$’s pm25_mean_2019, $x_i$ was B19083_001E:Gini Index, $\beta_0$ was an intercept, $\beta_1$ was the slope parameter of interest, and $\varepsilon_i$ was an error term assumed independent and identically distributed under the classical OLS model. The model was fit on complete cases for pm25_mean_2019, which is reflected by the fact that the appended fitted values ols_fitted_pm25 and residuals ols_residual_pm25 were non-null for 44 counties (Output File Statistics for counties_pm25_gini_2019_olsresid.gpkg).

Operationally, we fit the OLS model using the county dataset counties_pm25_gini_2019.gpkg and appended fitted values and residuals back to the county features as ols_fitted_pm25 and ols_residual_pm25, keyed by GEOID (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 4; output: counties_pm25_gini_2019_olsresid.gpkg). The pipeline also wrote tabular summaries to ols_pm25_gini_coefficients.csv and ols_pm25_gini_fit_stats.csv (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 4). The trace did not record additional regression diagnostics (e.g., heteroskedasticity checks, VIFs, or influential-point assessment); thus, model assessment in this workflow focused on spatial diagnostics rather than the full suite of OLS assumption checks, which we treated as a limitation for interpretability in spatially structured settings (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16].

To test whether unexplained spatial structure remained after the non-spatial baseline, we computed global Moran’s I for the OLS residual field ols_residual_pm25 using the same Queen contiguity weights keyed by GEOID (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 5; planned input: ca_county_queen_weights_2019.gpkg; output: ols_residuals_moransI_2019.csv). This diagnostic followed the standard logic that if residuals are spatially autocorrelated, the independence assumption underpinning conventional OLS inference is violated and spatial econometric specifications may be warranted (Anselin, 1988) [REF_17]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22]. While the research design anticipated fitting maximum-likelihood spatial lag and spatial error models, the provided execution trace block was truncated after the residual Moran’s I step; consequently, we did not report spatial-regression estimation details (e.g., SAR/SEM equations, estimator options) that were not verifiably executed in the trace.

Spatial weights construction (contiguity and KNN)

We operationalized county adjacency in two ways, corresponding to polygon-based and centroid-based proximity graphs commonly used in spatial analysis (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22]. First, Queen contiguity weights were constructed from county polygon boundaries and keyed by GEOID (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 6), producing ca_county_queen_weights_2019.gpkg in EPSG:4269 (Output File Statistics). Second, a K-nearest neighbors (KNN) weights file ca_county_knn_weights_2019.gpkg was produced with k = 5, constructed from county geometry centroids and keyed by GEOID (Research Plan; Output File Statistics report the file exists with EPSG:4269 and 58 rows). However, because the creation of the KNN weights file was not documented in the visible portion of the execution trace (the objective 2 trace was truncated), we reported only those aspects verifiable from output metadata: the file name, its existence, its CRS (EPSG:4269), and its record count (58) (Output File Statistics). We did not infer whether it was used in downstream modeling beyond what was explicitly recorded.

2.4 Computational Environment

All analyses were conducted in Python 3.13.12 on Linux 6.8.0-107-generic (analysis date UTC: 2026-04-22). Spatial data handling relied on geopandas=1.1.2, shapely=2.1.2, fiona=1.10.1, pyproj=3.7.2, and rasterio=1.5.0, while spatial weights and autocorrelation diagnostics used libpysal=4.14.1 and esda=2.9.0; regression modeling used statsmodels=0.14.6 alongside spatial econometrics libraries spreg=1.9.0 and spglm=1.1.0 (Computational Environment). Random seed(s) were not recorded, which limited strict bitwise reproducibility for any stochastic procedures (though the executed steps reported here were deterministic given fixed inputs).

y_i = \beta_0 + \beta_1 x_i + \varepsilon_i, \qquad \varepsilon_i \sim \mathcal{N}(0,\sigma^2),
$$
where $y_i$ was county $i$’s pm25_mean_2019, $x_i$ was B19083_001E:Gini Index, $\beta_0$ was an intercept, $\beta_1$ was the slope parameter of interest, and $\varepsilon_i$ was an error term assumed independent and identically distributed under the classical OLS model. The model was fit on complete cases for pm25_mean_2019, which is reflected by the fact that the appended fitted values ols_fitted_pm25 and residuals ols_residual_pm25 were non-null for 44 counties (Output File Statistics for counties_pm25_gini_2019_olsresid.gpkg).

Operationally, we fit the OLS model using the county dataset counties_pm25_gini_2019.gpkg and appended fitted values and residuals back to the county features as ols_fitted_pm25 and ols_residual_pm25, keyed by GEOID (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 4; output: counties_pm25_gini_2019_olsresid.gpkg). The pipeline also wrote tabular summaries to ols_pm25_gini_coefficients.csv and ols_pm25_gini_fit_stats.csv (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 4). The trace did not record additional regression diagnostics (e.g., heteroskedasticity checks, VIFs, or influential-point assessment); thus, model assessment in this workflow focused on spatial diagnostics rather than the full suite of OLS assumption checks, which we treated as a limitation for interpretability in spatially structured settings (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16].

To test whether unexplained spatial structure remained after the non-spatial baseline, we computed global Moran’s I for the OLS residual field ols_residual_pm25 using the same Queen contiguity weights keyed by GEOID (Execution Trace, Objective 2, step 5; planned input: ca_county_queen_weights_2019.gpkg; output: ols_residuals_moransI_2019.csv). This diagnostic followed the standard logic that if residuals are spatially autocorrelated, the independence assumption underpinning conventional OLS inference is violated and spatial econometric specifications may be warranted (Anselin, 1988) [REF_17]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22]. While the research design anticipated fitting maximum-likelihood spatial lag and spatial error models, the provided execution trace block was truncated after the residual Moran’s I step; consequently, we did not report spatial-regression estimation details (e.g., SAR/SEM equations, estimator options) that were not verifiably executed in the trace.

Spatial weights construction (contiguity and KNN)

We operationalized county adjacency in two ways, corresponding to polygon-based and centroid-based proximity graphs commonly used in spatial analysis (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22]. First, Queen contiguity weights were constructed from county polygon boundaries and keyed by GEOID (Execution Trace, Objective 1, step 6), producing ca_county_queen_weights_2019.gpkg in EPSG:4269 (Output File Statistics). Second, a K-nearest neighbors (KNN) weights file ca_county_knn_weights_2019.gpkg was produced with k = 5, constructed from county geometry centroids and keyed by GEOID (Research Plan; Output File Statistics report the file exists with EPSG:4269 and 58 rows). However, because the creation of the KNN weights file was not documented in the visible portion of the execution trace (the objective 2 trace was truncated), we reported only those aspects verifiable from output metadata: the file name, its existence, its CRS (EPSG:4269), and its record count (58) (Output File Statistics). We did not infer whether it was used in downstream modeling beyond what was explicitly recorded.

2.4 Computational Environment

All analyses were conducted in Python 3.13.12 on Linux 6.8.0-107-generic (analysis date UTC: 2026-04-22). Spatial data handling relied on geopandas=1.1.2, shapely=2.1.2, fiona=1.10.1, pyproj=3.7.2, and rasterio=1.5.0, while spatial weights and autocorrelation diagnostics used libpysal=4.14.1 and esda=2.9.0; regression modeling used statsmodels=0.14.6 alongside spatial econometrics libraries spreg=1.9.0 and spglm=1.1.0 (Computational Environment). Random seed(s) were not recorded, which limited strict bitwise reproducibility for any stochastic procedures (though the executed steps reported here were deterministic given fixed inputs).

3. Results

3.1 Overview

Analyses were conducted for California’s 58 counties (EPSG:4269), using a monitor-derived county annual mean PM2.5 exposure indicator for 2019 (pm25_mean_2019) and a county income-inequality measure (B19083_001E:Gini Index). After aggregating monitoring-site observations to counties and joining to county polygons, 44 of 58 counties (75.9%) had non-missing pm25_mean_2019, while 14 counties (24.1%) lacked monitor-derived annual PM2.5 under the specified filter (pm25_missing_2019 = TRUE). Consequently, inferential analyses involving PM2.5 (global Moran’s I for pm25_mean_2019, OLS, and spatial regressions) were effectively based on the 44 complete-case counties.

Across the 44 counties with observed PM2.5, county mean PM2.5 averaged 7.45 (SD = 2.14), with a range from 3.14 to 12.90. Monitoring coverage varied substantially: pm25_site_count_2019 averaged 3.39 sites per county (SD = 3.24; range: 1 to 19). For spatial dependence diagnostics and modeling, two neighborhood definitions were used as described in the Methods: Queen contiguity weights keyed by GEOID, and centroid-based K-nearest-neighbor weights (k = 5) keyed by GEOID.

3.2 Objective 1: Assess spatial clustering of county PM2.5 exposure in 2019

Using the Queen contiguity spatial weights described in Section 2.3, county neighborhood structure varied from 2 to 8 neighbors (mean = 4.79; median = 5.00), reflecting California’s heterogeneous county sizes and adjacency structure. Figure 2 visualizes the Queen contiguity graph (neighbor links and neighbor counts) used for the global spatial autocorrelation test.

As shown in Figure 2, neighbor connectivity was denser in parts of the state with smaller, more tightly tessellated counties, and sparser where counties were larger and fewer. This spatial weights structure provided the basis for evaluating whether county-level PM2.5 values were spatially patterned beyond what would be expected under spatial randomness.

Global Moran’s I results for pm25_mean_2019 are reported in Table 6. Moran’s I was positive and statistically distinguishable from the permutation reference distribution (I = 0.237; z = 2.39; p = 0.009; n = 44), indicating global positive spatial autocorrelation in county mean PM2.5 among counties with observed 2019 monitoring-derived values.

Table 6. Global Moran’s I (Queen contiguity; row-standardized) for county-level PM2.5 mean in 2019, reporting permutation-based variance, z-score, and p-value.

variable weights n permutations morans_I expected_I variance z_score p_value
pm25_mean_2019 queen 44 999 0.236979 -0.023256 0.012133 2.385176 0.009

Hypothesis Assessment (Objective 1). The global Moran’s I for county mean PM2.5 was positive (I = 0.237) and statistically significant under the permutation test (p = 0.009) for the 44 counties with observed 2019 PM2.5. This pattern was consistent with global positive spatial clustering rather than spatial randomness; therefore, the Objective 1 hypothesis was supported.

3.3 Objective 2: Estimate the PM2.5–Gini association and diagnose remaining spatial autocorrelation (including metro–nonmetro heterogeneity)

3.3.1 Baseline association (OLS) and residual spatial dependence

Using the OLS specification described in Section 2.3, county mean PM2.5 was regressed on the county Gini Index for the 44 complete-case counties. Coefficient estimates are reported in Table 9. The estimated slope for Gini was small relative to its uncertainty (β = 2.20, SE = 14.0, p = 0.876; 95% CI: −26.1, 30.5), with an intercept of 6.44 (SE = 6.46, p = 0.325). Model fit statistics (Table 10) indicated essentially no explained variance (R² = 0.0006; adjusted R² = −0.023), with RMSE = 2.12.

Table 9. OLS coefficients for PM2.5 (2019) regressed on county Gini Index.

variable coef std_err p_value ci_low ci_high
const 6.438133 6.460260 0.324678 -6.599201 19.475466
B19083_001E:Gini Index 2.200437 14.006256 0.875916 -26.065332 30.466207

Table 10. OLS fit statistics for PM2.5 (2019) ~ Gini Index model.

n R2 adj_R2 AIC BIC RMSE
44 0.000587 -0.023208 194.943296 198.511675 2.118846

Figure 3 maps the OLS fitted values and residuals attached to counties, supporting the subsequent residual spatial autocorrelation diagnostic.

As displayed in Figure 3, fitted values were narrowly distributed (reflecting the weak estimated association in Table 9), while residuals exhibited spatial patterning in several parts of the state rather than appearing spatially alternating at fine scales. This visual pattern was evaluated formally using global Moran’s I on the OLS residuals.

Permutation-based Moran’s I for the OLS residuals was positive and statistically significant (I = 0.242; z = 2.32; p = 0.015; n = 44), indicating remaining residual spatial autocorrelation after controlling for Gini in a non-spatial regression (Table 4).

Table 4. Global Moran’s I (permutation-based) for spatial autocorrelation in OLS residuals of PM2.5 across California counties using Queen contiguity.

variable morans_I z_sim p_sim n
ols_residual_pm25 0.242459 2.321096 0.015 44

3.3.2 Spatial regression sensitivity (SAR and SEM)

Given the residual autocorrelation under Queen contiguity (Table 4), a maximum-likelihood spatial lag (SAR) model with Queen weights (Section 2.3) was estimated for PM2.5 on Gini. In the SAR model (Table 12), the Gini coefficient remained imprecisely estimated (β = 7.53, SE = 12.6, z = 0.597, p = 0.551). The spatially lagged PM2.5 term was positive and statistically significant (β = 0.417, SE = 0.158, z = 2.64, p = 0.008), consistent with positive spatial dependence in PM2.5 after accounting for Gini. Model fit statistics from the same output indicated pseudo-R² = 0.198 and AIC = 192.15 (n = 44).

Table 12. Maximum-likelihood spatial lag (SAR) model results for county PM2.5 (2019) regressed on Gini Index with Queen contiguity weights (GEOID-keyed).

section variable estimate std_err z_value p_value
parameters CONSTANT 0.830436 5.907173 0.140581 0.888201
parameters B19083_001E:Gini Index 7.531772 12.622788 0.596681 0.550721
parameters W_pm25_mean_2019 0.416924 0.157767 2.642652 0.008226

fit_stats bic_schwarz 197.506925 NaN NaN NaN
fit_stats pseudo_r2 0.197621 NaN NaN NaN
fit_stats sigma2 3.817439 NaN NaN NaN

A centroid-based KNN (k = 5) neighborhood definition (Section 2.3) was also constructed for an SEM sensitivity analysis; Figure 1 depicts the KNN graph used for that specification.

Figure 1 shows a uniform neighbor count by construction (k = 5) and long centroid-to-centroid links for large, sparsely partitioned counties, reflecting the geometry-driven connectivity imposed by KNN weights. Under the SEM specification estimated with this KNN structure (Table 7), the Gini coefficient was again imprecise (β = 14.6, SE = 12.5, z = 1.17, p = 0.244). The estimated spatial error parameter was positive (λ = 0.582), while overall model fit remained low (pseudo-R² = 0.0006; AIC = 186.48; n = 44).

Table 7. Spatial error (SEM) maximum-likelihood estimates for PM2.5 (2019) regressed on county Gini Index (KNN k=5 weights, keyed by GEOID).

B19083_001E:Gini Index 14.570880 12.506137 1.165098 0.243979 0.582317 186.48456 190.052939 -91.24228 0.000587 44 2

3.3.3 Urban–rural heterogeneity (Metro2013 stratification and interaction models)

To evaluate whether the PM2.5–Gini relationship differed by metropolitan status, a nonparametric association was first summarized via Spearman rank correlation stratified by Metro2013 (Table 5). The estimated correlations were near zero in metro counties (ρ = −0.0204, p = 0.910; n = 33) and modestly positive in non-metro counties (ρ = 0.155, p = 0.650; n = 11), with wide uncertainty as reflected by the non-significant p-values in both strata.

Table 5. Spearman rank correlation between 2019 county PM2.5 and Gini Index, stratified by Metro2013 status, with sample sizes.

group Metro2013 n spearman_rho spearman_p x y
metro 1 33 -0.020388 0.910337 pm25_mean_2019 B19083_001E:Gini Index
nonmetro 0 11 0.154545 0.650034 pm25_mean_2019 B19083_001E:Gini Index

An OLS interaction model (Section 2.3) with main effects for Gini and Metro2013 plus their interaction (gini_x_metro) also yielded imprecise estimates for all terms (Table 11). The interaction coefficient was negative (β = −16.5, SE = 28.8, p = 0.571; 95% CI: −74.6, 41.7), and neither the Gini main effect (β = 11.5, SE = 24.6, p = 0.643) nor the Metro main effect (β = 9.77, SE = 13.2, p = 0.464) was statistically distinguishable from zero.

Table 11. OLS coefficient table for PM2.5 (2019) regressed on Gini Index, Metro2013, and their interaction (gini_x_metro).

variable coef std_err p_value ci_low_95 ci_high_95
const 0.528214 11.282688 0.962893 -22.274949 23.331376
B19083_001E:Gini Index 11.483663 24.602370 0.643196 -38.239582 61.206907
Metro2013 9.773199 13.228208 0.464333 -16.962007 36.508405
gini_x_metro -16.455723 28.785020 0.570740 -74.632418 41.720971

A spatial lag (SAR) interaction model using Queen contiguity weights (Section 2.3) similarly produced imprecise coefficients for Gini, Metro, and their interaction (Table 8): Gini (β = 29.1, SE = 21.9, p = 0.183), Metro (β = 17.1, SE = 11.8, p = 0.147), and interaction (β = −33.1, SE = 25.6, p = 0.196). In contrast, the spatial autoregressive parameter remained positive and statistically significant (ρ = 0.393, SE = 0.155, p = 0.0109), while the reported pseudo-R² was 0.320 (n = 44).

Table 8. Maximum-likelihood spatial lag (SAR) model coefficients for county PM2.5 (2019) with Gini, Metro2013, and their interaction using Queen contiguity weights keyed by GEOID.

variable coef std_err z p_value n k aic bic pseudo_r2
CONSTANT -10.275258 10.139456 -1.013393 0.310872 44 5 186.393056 195.314004 0.319882
B19083_001E:Gini Index 29.133698 21.890438 1.330887 0.183226 44 5 186.393056 195.314004 0.319882
Metro2013 17.068384 11.770344 1.450118 0.147026 44 5 186.393056 195.314004 0.319882
gini_x_metro -33.129736 25.625393 -1.292848 0.196064 44 5 186.393056 195.314004 0.319882
rho 0.393330 0.154540 2.545174 0.010922 44 5 186.393056 195.314004 0.319882

Hypothesis Assessment (Objective 2). In the baseline OLS model, the estimated association between county Gini and mean PM2.5 was positive but highly uncertain (β = 2.20, SE = 14.0; p = 0.876), with near-zero explanatory power (R² = 0.0006). However, OLS residuals were spatially autocorrelated under Queen contiguity (Moran’s I = 0.242; p = 0.015), and spatial lag models estimated a positive and statistically significant spatial dependence parameter (e.g., $W y$ coefficient = 0.417, p = 0.008 in Table 12; ρ = 0.393, p = 0.0109 in Table 8). Stratified and interaction results by Metro2013 did not provide statistically distinguishable evidence of differential PM2.5–Gini association by metropolitan status (Table 5; Table 11; Table 8). Therefore, the hypothesis was partially supported: residual spatial autocorrelation was supported, whereas a positive county-level PM2.5–Gini association (and its metro–nonmetro differentiation) was not supported by the estimated coefficients and uncertainty intervals in these models.

4. Discussion

4.1 Principal Findings

Three headline patterns emerged from the county-scale analysis of California in 2019. First, monitor-derived annual mean PM2.5 was not spatially random across counties; instead, it exhibited statistically detectable positive global clustering under Queen contiguity. Second, the county Gini Index was not meaningfully associated with county PM2.5 in the bivariate OLS specification and remained imprecise in spatial-regression sensitivities; however, the OLS residual field retained significant spatial autocorrelation, indicating that the dominant structure in the county PM2.5 surface was spatial rather than explained by inequality alone. Third, tests for urban–rural effect modification (Metro2013 stratification and interaction modeling) did not provide evidence that the PM2.5–Gini association systematically differed between metro and non-metro counties. The remainder of the Discussion interprets these patterns in terms of spatial exposure processes and model diagnostics, revisits the hypotheses, and outlines implications, limitations, and next steps.

4.2 Interpretation

4.2.1 Spatially structured PM2.5 as an “airshed-and-systems” phenomenon (and why this supports H1)

Pattern. County-level PM2.5 showed positive global spatial autocorrelation, and the spatial patterning was not eliminated by a non-spatial regression on Gini. In other words, counties tended to resemble their neighbors in PM2.5 levels, and that resemblance persisted in the residuals after accounting for inequality in a simple way.

Mechanism (with boundary conditions). A plausible mechanism is that county PM2.5 is shaped by processes that operate at regional spatial supports larger than counties—shared meteorology, topographic basins, cross-boundary transport, and correlated emissions systems (freight corridors, port complexes, regional labor and commuting sheds). In such settings, adjacency-based similarity is expected because neighboring jurisdictions often occupy the same airshed and share infrastructure and policy regimes, producing spatially autocorrelated exposure fields even when counties are the analytic unit. This interpretation is consistent with the general warning that geographically indexed variables frequently violate independence due to spatial autocorrelation, and that standard regressions can leave structured errors if spatial dependence is unmodeled (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]. It also matches the spatial-econometric framing that dependence may enter through the outcome itself (a diffusion-like process captured by a spatial lag) or through spatially correlated unobservables (captured by a spatial error process), motivating diagnostics and alternative specifications (Anselin, 1988) [REF_17]; (Elhorst, 2011) [REF_18]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22].

This mechanism has clear boundary conditions. It should be weaker when the areal units poorly approximate the relevant physical transport domain (e.g., when an airshed cuts across “adjacent” counties but not others, or when mountain barriers create sharp discontinuities despite contiguity). It may also be weaker when major contributors to PM2.5 are highly localized point sources with steep near-source gradients that do not align with county boundaries, or when year-to-year episodic events (e.g., wildfire smoke) dominate exposures in ways that create non-contiguous similarity.

Engagement with prior literature. The observed global clustering directly aligns with the methodological expectation—well established across applied spatial sciences—that exposure-like outcomes are spatially dependent, and that diagnosing this dependence is a prerequisite for defensible inference (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]; (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19]. At the same time, the result is substantively consistent with environmental-justice framings that treat pollution burdens as place-based and system-produced rather than idiosyncratic (Banzhaf et al., 2019) [REF_7]. In that sense, H1 was supported: the county PM2.5 surface was spatially clustered in a way that is consistent with shared regional processes.

A study-specific qualifier is that clustering was evaluated on the subset of counties with monitor-derived PM2.5 summaries, and monitoring coverage was uneven across space. Spatial autocorrelation could therefore partly reflect where monitors exist (and how many), not only where pollution is high—an issue returned to in §4.5 as a construct-validity concern.

4.2.2 Inequality as a weak county-scale predictor of PM2.5 in 2019 (why H2 was only partially supported)

Pattern. The bivariate OLS association between county Gini and county PM2.5 was near-zero relative to uncertainty, with essentially no explained variance, while the OLS residuals remained positively spatially autocorrelated. Spatial-regression sensitivities did not yield a precise Gini association either; by contrast, the spatially lagged PM2.5 term in the SAR specification was statistically significant, indicating that spatial dependence in PM2.5 was a prominent feature of the data-generating structure at the county scale.

Mechanism (with boundary conditions). One plausible mechanism is scale mismatch between (i) the social process measured by the Gini Index and (ii) the emissions and atmospheric processes shaping ambient PM2.5. The county Gini Index captures within-county income dispersion, not poverty concentration, regulatory capacity, proximity to specific sources, or the siting and routing decisions that often drive environmental-justice disparities. If the primary correlates of PM2.5 are basin-scale meteorology, goods movement networks, and regional industrial structure, then adjacency and shared regional context can dominate the spatial signal, leaving Gini weak as a standalone predictor in a county-level cross-section. This mechanism would be less applicable in contexts where inequality is tightly coupled to land-use segregation and source proximity at the same spatial support as the exposure measure (e.g., within metros at tract or block-group scales), or where political economy processes link inequality to uneven enforcement or infrastructure in ways that strongly pattern ambient concentrations across the same units. In short, the mechanism predicts that Gini’s explanatory power should increase when the outcome and the inequality process are measured at scales where sorting and siting occur, and decrease when the unit is too coarse.

Engagement with prior literature. Environmental-justice research robustly documents that pollution burdens frequently align with social stratification (Banzhaf et al., 2019) [REF_7], but it also emphasizes that the relevant stratification dimensions and mechanisms are often place-specific (e.g., racialized segregation, zoning, and source proximity) and may not be well summarized by a single county-level inequality index. The present findings therefore do not contradict EJ theory so much as specify a boundary condition: at the county scale in California in 2019, within-county income inequality (Gini) did not co-vary strongly with ambient PM2.5 means, while spatial structure did. This is also consistent with the spatial-econometric caution that a statistically “weak” covariate can co-exist with strong spatial dependence, and that unmodeled dependence can manifest as residual autocorrelation rather than as an inflated coefficient (Anselin, 1988) [REF_17]; (Unknown, 2015) [REF_22]. Methodologically, the significant residual Moran’s I and the significant spatial-lag term align with the motivation to treat “space” as part of the data-generating process rather than a nuisance, and to explicitly test whether baseline models leave spatially patterned errors (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19]; (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16].

In hypothesis terms, H2 comprised two claims: (i) a positive PM2.5–Gini association and (ii) spatial autocorrelation in OLS residuals indicating the need for spatial specifications. The evidence supported the second claim but not the first. Thus, H2 was partially supported: spatial dependence remained after OLS, but higher Gini was not associated with higher county PM2.5 in this cross-section.

One unexpected aspect—given a common intuition that inequality and environmental burden should align—was how completely the bivariate county association failed to emerge. A concrete explanation is measurement and support: “county mean PM2.5” aggregates heterogeneous within-county exposure patterns, while the Gini Index aggregates heterogeneous within-county income distributions. When both are aggregated, within-county sorting mechanisms central to EJ (e.g., near-roadway gradients and neighborhood-level segregation) can be averaged away, biasing associations toward zero (an ecological/aggregation attenuation). Additionally, 2019 is a single year; if episodic PM2.5 drivers (including wildfire smoke) affected multiple neighboring counties simultaneously, they would strengthen spatial clustering but not necessarily strengthen covariance with income inequality.

4.2.3 Metro–nonmetro heterogeneity: limited evidence of effect modification (why H3 was not supported)

Pattern. Stratified nonparametric associations were weak in both metro and non-metro groups, and interaction models did not yield statistically distinguishable metro-specific slopes for Gini. The spatial lag interaction specification similarly did not identify a clear or precise interaction pattern, even though it did detect positive spatial dependence via the spatial autoregressive parameter.

Mechanism (with boundary conditions). One plausible mechanism for the absence of clear effect modification is that the main determinants of county mean PM2.5—regional transport, basin effects, and broad emissions structure—operate in both metro and non-metro contexts, compressing differences in how inequality maps to the county mean. Additionally, “Metro2013” is a coarse indicator; within the metro category, California contains counties with very different emissions profiles and governance environments, and within the non-metro category, counties can differ sharply in topography and wildfire susceptibility. If the true heterogeneity is organized along corridors/airsheds or sectoral emissions (e.g., ports, agriculture, wildfire-prone forested regions) rather than along a binary metro–nonmetro split, then interaction with Metro2013 will be an underpowered proxy.

This mechanism would not apply if metro status were tightly aligned with the relevant exposure-production processes (e.g., if metro counties were uniformly traffic-dominated and non-metro counties uniformly background-dominated) and if inequality were linked to exposure through a common within-unit mechanism that differs systematically by metro status (e.g., consistent patterns of residential sorting and source siting). Under those conditions, an interaction might emerge more clearly.

Engagement with prior literature. The motivation for H3 drew on the idea that California’s urban form and transportation systems can produce sharp spatial gradients (Severen, 2021) [REF_15] and that decarbonization planning foregrounds distributional questions that plausibly vary across urban contexts (Hettinger et al., 2021) [REF_4]. The present findings suggest that such heterogeneity may not be well captured by a county-level metro/non-metro interaction on a single inequality indicator in a single year. In hypothesis terms, H3 was not supported: the data did not provide evidence that the PM2.5–Gini association differed systematically by Metro2013 status at the county scale.

A study-specific limitation qualifying this interpretation is statistical power: the non-metro stratum contained relatively few counties with complete PM2.5 data, so even moderate differences in association would be difficult to detect reliably. This is a statistical-conclusion-validity concern rather than substantive evidence of “no heterogeneity.”

4.3 Closing the Loop with the Research Gap

The Introduction identified a gap at the intersection of (i) county-scale environmental exposure inequality, (ii) income inequality as a candidate correlate, and (iii) explicit diagnosis and modeling of spatial dependence—particularly in a California-relevant setting where cross-boundary processes plausibly shape exposure (Watts et al., 2020) [REF_6]; (Banzhaf et al., 2019) [REF_7]. Prior gap-anchoring studies addressed adjacent topics but not this combination: micro-level productivity impacts in a non-U.S. context without county-scale spatial structuring (Chang et al., 2019) [REF_8], health impacts of wildfire smoke without linking ambient PM2.5 patterns to inequality and spatial dependence (Chen et al., 2021) [REF_10], and macroeconomic inequality relationships without spatially explicit environmental exposure processes (Bleaney and Nishiyama, 2004) [REF_1]. This study advances the gap by showing that, for California counties in 2019 with monitor-derived PM2.5, the dominant empirical regularity is spatial clustering (and persistent residual spatial autocorrelation), whereas the bivariate association with income inequality is weak and imprecise and does not robustly vary by metro status. The gap is not “closed,” however, because the evidence remains cross-sectional, county-aggregated, and subject to monitoring coverage limitations; the most plausible remaining uncertainty is whether inequality–exposure relationships emerge at finer spatial supports, over multiple years, or with exposure surfaces that better capture within-county variation.

4.4 Implications

The results carry two main implications for GIScience-oriented environmental inequality research and for applied environmental-justice screening at the county scale.

First, the findings reinforce that spatial dependence is not an optional technical add-on but a substantive feature of county-level air-pollution surfaces. The presence of both global clustering in PM2.5 and significant residual spatial autocorrelation after OLS implies that non-spatial regressions risk overstating how much a single socioeconomic covariate “accounts for” geographic variation, while simultaneously understating the role of shared regional processes. This supports the methodological position that diagnostics (e.g., Moran’s I on outcomes and residuals) should be treated as part of the main inferential workflow, consistent with spatial-econometric guidance on specification and error dependence (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]; (Anselin, 1988) [REF_17]; (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19]. Substantively, the significant spatial lag component suggests that county PM2.5 is better understood as a regional system, which aligns with policy framings that emphasize cross-jurisdictional coordination for environmental justice under energy transition pathways (Hettinger et al., 2021) [REF_4].

Second, the weak county-level association between Gini and PM2.5 cautions against using income inequality alone—especially a within-county dispersion measure—as a screening proxy for ambient PM2.5 burdens at this spatial scale. Environmental-justice analysis often depends on choosing indicators that match hypothesized mechanisms; here, the evidence suggests that if the goal is to anticipate where PM2.5 is higher, then spatial context (neighbors/airsheds) and emissions-system correlates may be more informative at the county scale than the Gini Index by itself, even if inequality remains highly relevant for within-county exposure disparities. This implication complements broader EJ perspectives emphasizing that “race, place, and pollution” relationships are mechanism-specific and scale-sensitive rather than guaranteed to appear under any aggregation (Banzhaf et al., 2019) [REF_7].

4.5 Limitations and Validity

Several limitations qualify the interpretation and point to the kinds of validity threats most relevant for this study. The first is incomplete and spatially uneven exposure measurement, as 14 of 58 counties lacked monitor-derived PM2.5 summaries under the specified filter. This is a construct-validity and external-validity concern: monitor presence is not random with respect to population density, regulatory attention, or expected pollution, so complete-case inference may over-represent more monitored (often more urban) counties. The likely bias is toward underrepresenting exposure patterns in sparsely monitored areas and potentially distorting the inferred spatial structure if missingness clusters geographically; in turn, this could either inflate or attenuate Moran’s I and regression relationships depending on where missing counties fall in the PM2.5 surface. Addressing this would require either modeled exposure surfaces with wall-to-wall coverage or an explicit missing-data strategy that incorporates monitoring propensity.

Second, the analysis relied on county means of PM2.5 and county-level Gini, which raises a classic ecological/aggregation limitation. This threatens construct validity (county mean PM2.5 is not personal exposure and obscures within-county gradients) and internal validity for mechanistic claims about inequality processes, because the social sorting and source-proximity mechanisms emphasized in environmental-justice theory often operate at neighborhood scales. The likely effect is attenuation of associations (bias toward zero) if inequality–exposure linkages are primarily within-county rather than between-county. A different design using census-tract exposure estimates and tract-level socioeconomic measures would better align the measurement supports with plausible mechanisms.

Third, the design was cross-sectional (single year), limiting temporal generalization and raising statistical-conclusion and internal-validity concerns. A single year can be influenced by episodic conditions (including smoke events) that create broad spatial coherence and thereby strengthen spatial dependence while weakening correlation with slowly moving socioeconomic measures such as inequality. This temporal mismatch could bias the Gini association toward zero and complicate interpretation of spatial parameters as stable properties rather than year-specific realizations. Multi-year panels or repeated cross-sections would help distinguish persistent spatial structure from year-specific shocks, consistent with broader spatial-econometric emphasis on dynamic and panel approaches when processes evolve (Elhorst, 2011) [REF_18].

Fourth, the study’s inference depends on spatial-weights specification (Queen contiguity versus centroid KNN). Although the analysis intentionally examined more than one neighborhood definition, weights remain a modeling assumption, and different weights can imply different “channels” of dependence (shared borders versus distance-based proximity). This is a model-specification and statistical-conclusion-validity limitation: coefficients on spatial parameters and diagnostics of residual autocorrelation can be sensitive to the chosen graph. The directional implication is not necessarily biased coefficients in one direction, but potential instability of inference about whether dependence is best represented as lag or error correlation. More extensive sensitivity analysis across plausible weights (and ideally weights tied to atmospheric transport or commuting/economic linkages) would better match the “space is more than geography” principle emphasized in applied spatial econometrics (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19].

4.6 Future Research

Future work can build directly on the limitations and the unresolved questions raised by the findings. First, to address monitoring missingness and the construct-validity limitations of monitor-derived county means, a follow-up study could use wall-to-wall exposure estimates (e.g., satellite-informed or fused PM2.5 surfaces) aggregated consistently to counties and to sub-county units. This would allow testing whether the observed global clustering persists when the exposure surface is not conditioned on monitor placement, and whether inequality associations emerge when within-county variation is represented.

Second, to evaluate whether inequality–PM2.5 relationships are primarily within-county rather than between-county, future analysis should shift to a finer spatial support (census tracts or block groups) and pair PM2.5 estimates with socioeconomic indicators more directly tied to EJ mechanisms (e.g., poverty, racialized segregation proxies, housing burden), while still applying spatial diagnostics and spatial regression or spatially robust inference (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]; (DellaVigna et al., 2025) [REF_3]. This would directly test whether the null county-level association reflects aggregation attenuation.

Third, to separate persistent spatial structure from year-specific shocks, future research should use a multi-year panel (or repeated annual cross-sections) spanning years with differing wildfire activity and regulatory conditions, estimating dynamic or panel spatial models where appropriate (Elhorst, 2011) [REF_18]. Such a design would clarify whether metro–nonmetro differences emerge over time, and whether spatial dependence parameters are stable features of California’s PM2.5 geography or contingent on particular years’ atmospheric conditions.

📄 Manuscript section: discussion

4. Discussion

4.1 Principal Findings

Three headline patterns emerged from the county-scale analysis of California in 2019. First, monitor-derived annual mean PM2.5 was not spatially random across counties; instead, it exhibited statistically detectable positive global clustering under Queen contiguity. A single year can be influenced by episodic conditions (including smoke events) that create broad spatial coherence and thereby strengthen spatial dependence while weakening correlation with slowly moving socioeconomic measures such as inequality. This temporal mismatch could bias the Gini association toward zero and complicate interpretation of spatial parameters as stable properties rather than year-specific realizations. Multi-year panels or repeated cross-sections would help distinguish persistent spatial structure from year-specific shocks, consistent with broader spatial-econometric emphasis on dynamic and panel approaches when processes evolve (Elhorst, 2011) [REF_18].

Fourth, the study’s inference depends on spatial-weights specification (Queen contiguity versus centroid KNN). Although the analysis intentionally examined more than one neighborhood definition, weights remain a modeling assumption, and different weights can imply different “channels” of dependence (shared borders versus distance-based proximity). This is a model-specification and statistical-conclusion-validity limitation: coefficients on spatial parameters and diagnostics of residual autocorrelation can be sensitive to the chosen graph. The directional implication is not necessarily biased coefficients in one direction, but potential instability of inference about whether dependence is best represented as lag or error correlation. More extensive sensitivity analysis across plausible weights (and ideally weights tied to atmospheric transport or commuting/economic linkages) would better match the “space is more than geography” principle emphasized in applied spatial econometrics (Beck et al., 2006) [REF_19].

4.6 Future Research

Future work can build directly on the limitations and the unresolved questions raised by the findings. First, to address monitoring missingness and the construct-validity limitations of monitor-derived county means, a follow-up study could use wall-to-wall exposure estimates (e.g., satellite-informed or fused PM2.5 surfaces) aggregated consistently to counties and to sub-county units. This would allow testing whether the observed global clustering persists when the exposure surface is not conditioned on monitor placement, and whether inequality associations emerge when within-county variation is represented.

Second, to evaluate whether inequality–PM2.5 relationships are primarily within-county rather than between-county, future analysis should shift to a finer spatial support (census tracts or block groups) and pair PM2.5 estimates with socioeconomic indicators more directly tied to EJ mechanisms (e.g., poverty, racialized segregation proxies, housing burden), while still applying spatial diagnostics and spatial regression or spatially robust inference (Dormann et al., 2007) [REF_16]; (DellaVigna et al., 2025) [REF_3]. This would directly test whether the null county-level association reflects aggregation attenuation.

Third, to separate persistent spatial structure from year-specific shocks, future research should use a multi-year panel (or repeated annual cross-sections) spanning years with differing wildfire activity and regulatory conditions, estimating dynamic or panel spatial models where appropriate (Elhorst, 2011) [REF_18]. Such a design would clarify whether metro–nonmetro differences emerge over time, and whether spatial dependence parameters are stable features of California’s PM2.5 geography or contingent on particular years’ atmospheric conditions.

[Conclusion] generating (no tool calls)...

5. Conclusion

Prior work had established that air-pollution fields and many place-based social indicators often violate independence because they are spatially structured; this study shows that, for California counties in 2019, monitor-derived county mean PM2.5 indeed exhibited statistically detectable positive spatial clustering and that this clustering persisted even after conditioning on a commonly used county inequality metric. In this setting, spatial dependence was not a secondary diagnostic detail but a defining feature of the county PM2.5 surface, while the bivariate PM2.5–Gini relationship was weak and imprecise across non-spatial and spatial specifications. The central advance is therefore a clarified boundary condition for county-scale environmental inequality inference in California: unequal exposure at this support appears more consistent with regional, spatially correlated processes than with cross-county variation in within-county income dispersion as summarized by the Gini index.

At a higher level, the results indicate three connected takeaways. County PM2.5 values tended to resemble those of neighboring counties, and the residual field from a non-spatial regression retained spatial autocorrelation, underscoring that “space” remained in the error structure when not modeled explicitly. In contrast, income inequality measured by the county Gini index did not co-vary meaningfully with county mean PM2.5, and evidence for urban–rural effect modification was not compelling, suggesting that the absence of a clear PM2.5–Gini pattern was not simply a metro-versus-nonmetro phenomenon in these data. Hypothesis tests aligned with these themes: H1 was supported (global PM2.5 clustering), H2 was partially supported (residual spatial autocorrelation but not a positive PM2.5–Gini association), and H3 was not supported (no systematic metro–nonmetro difference in the PM2.5–Gini association).

These claims are limited to a cross-sectional county analysis of California in 2019, with inferential models estimated on the 44 counties that had monitor-derived annual PM2.5 means under the study’s filtering and aggregation, and they describe associations rather than causal effects. The immediate next step enabled by this work is to evaluate whether the same dominance of spatial dependence over a county Gini signal persists when PM2.5 exposure is represented with spatially complete surfaces (reducing monitor-driven missingness) and when the analysis is repeated at finer geographic supports where sorting and source proximity operate.

5. Conclusion

Prior work had established that air-pollution fields and many place-based social indicators often violate independence because they are spatially structured; this study shows that, for California counties in 2019, monitor-derived county mean PM2.5 indeed exhibited statistically detectable positive spatial clustering and that this clustering persisted even after conditioning on a commonly used county inequality metric. The central advance is therefore a clarified boundary condition for county-scale environmental inequality inference in California: unequal exposure at this support appears more consistent with regional, spatially correlated processes than with cross-county variation in within-county income dispersion as summarized by the Gini index.

At a higher level, the results indicate three connected takeaways. County PM2.5 values tended to resemble those of neighboring counties, and the residual field from a non-spatial regression retained spatial autocorrelation, underscoring that “space” remained in the error structure when not modeled explicitly. In contrast, income inequality measured by the county Gini index did not co-vary meaningfully with county mean PM2.5, and evidence for urban–rural effect modification was not compelling, suggesting that the absence of a clear PM2.5–Gini pattern was not simply a metro-versus-nonmetro phenomenon in these data.
